                  . John 1:18: "No one has ever seen God, but God the One
                    and Only, who is at the Father's side, has made him
                    known."
          02. Mental Attributes
              i. Knowledge (Omniscience)
                  . Romans 11:33-36: "Oh, the depth of the riches of the
                    wisdom and knowledge of God! How unsearchable his
                    judgments, and his paths beyond tracing out! 34 "Who has
                    known the mind of the Lord? Or who has been his
                    counselor?" 35 "Who has ever given to God, that God
                    should repay him?" 36 For from him and through him and
                    to him are all things. To him be the glory forever!
                    Amen."
             ii. Wisdom
                  . Romans 11:33-36
            iii. Truthfulness (and Faithfulness)
                  . John 17:17: "Sanctify them by the truth; your word is
                    truth."
                  . Exodus 34:6-7: "And he passed in front of Moses,
                    proclaiming, 'The LORD, the LORD, the compassionate and
                    gracious God, slow to anger, abounding in love and
                    faithfulness, 7 maintaining love to thousands, and
                    forgiving wickedness, rebellion and sin. Yet he does not
                    leave the guilty unpunished; he punishes the children
                    and their children for the sin of the fathers to the
                    third and fourth generation.'"
          03. Moral Attributes
              i. Goodness
                  . Psalm 145:9: "The LORD is good to all; he has compassion
                    on all he has made."
             ii. Love
                  . Exodus 34:6-7
            iii. Mercy (Grace, Patience)
                  . Exodus 34:6-7
             iv. Holiness
                  . Exodus 15:11: "Who among the gods is like you, O LORD?
                    Who is like you-majestic in holiness, awesome in glory,
                    working wonders?"
              v. Peace (or Order)
                  . Hebrews 13:20-21: "May the God of peace, who through the
                    blood of the eternal covenant brought back from the dead
                    our Lord Jesus, that great Shepherd of the sheep, 21
                    equip you with everything good for doing his will, and
                    may he work in us what is pleasing to him, through Jesus
                    Christ, to whom be glory for ever and ever. Amen."
             vi. Righteousness (or Justice)
                  . Exodus 34:6-7
            vii. Jealousy
                  . Exodus 20:4-6: "You shall not make for yourself an idol
                    in the form of anything in heaven above or on the earth
                    beneath or in the waters below. 5 You shall not bow down
                    to them or worship them; for I, the LORD your God, am a
                    jealous God, punishing the children for the sin of the
                    fathers to the third and fourth generation of those who
                    hate me, 6 but showing love to a thousand generations of
                    those who love me and keep my commandments."
           viii. Wrath
                  . Exodus 34:6-7
          04. Attributes of Purpose
              i. Will
                  . Ephesians 1:11: "In him we were also chosen, having been
                    predestined according to the plan of him who works out
                    everything in conformity with the purpose of his
                    will...."
             ii. Freedom
                  . Psalm 115:3: "Our God is in heaven; he does whatever
                    pleases him."
            iii. Omnipotence (and Sovereignty)
                  . Ephesians 1:11
          05. "Summary" Attributes
              i. Perfection
                  . Matthew 5:48: "Be perfect, therefore, as your heavenly
                    Father is perfect."
             ii. Blessedness
                  . Ephesians 1:3: "Praise be to the God and Father of our
                    Lord Jesus Christ, who has blessed us in the heavenly
                    realms with every spiritual blessing in Christ."
            iii. Beauty
                  . Exodus 15:11
             iv. Glory
                  . Exodus 15:11
        b. Which are incommunicable
          01. Self-Existence
              i. John 5:26: "For as the Father has life in himself, so he
                 has granted the Son to have life in himself."
          02. Eternity
              i. Psalm 90:2: "...from everlasting to everlasting you are
                 God."
          03. Immutability of God
              i. Malachi 3:6: "I the LORD do not change. So you, O
                 descendants of Jacob, are not destroyed."
          04. Omnipresence
              i. Psalm 139:7: "Where can I go from your Spirit? Where can I
                 flee from your presence?"
          05. Unity of God
              i. Deuteronomy 6:4: "Hear, O Israel: The LORD our God, the
                 LORD is one."


     4. What are the decrees of God?
        a. WSC 7: "The decrees of God are, his eternal purpose, according
           to the counsel of his will, whereby, for his own glory, he hath
           foreordained whatsoever comes to pass."
          01. Ps. 33:11; Isa. 14:24; Acts 2:23; Eph. 1:11-12


     5. What is the difference between infralapsarian and supralapsarian
        view of God's decrees?
        a. "The terminology relates to whether logically, the decree to
           save comes before or after the decree to permit the fall. The
           positions also differ on whether the atonement was for all or
           only for those chosen to be saved."[35]
        b. Supralapsarianism
          01. "Supralapsarians include the decree to create and to permit
              the fall in the decree of predestination...."[36]
          02. The order of decrees are as follows:[37]
              i. The decree to save some and condemn others.
             ii. The decree to create both the elect and the reprobate.
            iii. The decree to permit the fall of both classes.
             iv. The decree to provide salvation only for the elect.
        c. Infralapsarianism
          01. "Infralapsarians refer [the decree to create and to permit
              the fall] to the decree of God in general, and exclude it
              from the special decree of predestination."[38]


          02. The order of decrees are as follows:[39]
              i. The decree to create human beings.
             ii. The decree to permit the fall.
            iii. The decree to save some and condemn others.
             iv. The decree to provide salvation only for the elect.
        d. With regard to infralapsarianism versus supralapsarianism, among
           other things, Berkhof says, "They consider the same mystery from
           different points of view, the one fixing its attention on the
           ideal or teleological; the other, on the historical, order of
           the decrees. To a certain extent they can and must go hand in
           hand. Both find support in Scripture. Supralapsarianism in those
           passages which stress the sovereignty of God, and
           Infralapsarianism in those which emphasize the mercy and justice
           of God, in connection with election and reprobation...Our
           confessional standards embody the infralapsarian position, but
           do not condemn Supralapsarianism. It was felt that this view was
           not necessarily inconsistent with Reformed theology."[40]


     6. What is an Amyraldian view of God's decrees?
        a. Amyraldianism is the "system of Reformed theology propounded by
           the French theologian Moise Amyraut and associates at the Saumur
           Academey in the seventeenth century...Amyraut insisted that the
           chief doctrine of Christian theology is not predesination but
           the faith which justifies...Amyraut's covenant theology-
           particularly his division of the covenant of grace into a
           universal conditional covenant and a particular unconditional
           covenant-provided the basis for the unique feature of
           Amyraldianism, namely, the doctrine of hypothetical universal
           predestination...Amyraldianism [also] postulated a universalist
           design in the atonement and a particular application of its
           benefits...Some later Reformed theologians such as Charles
           Hodge, W.G.T. Shedd, and B.B. Warfield insisted that
           Amyraldianism was an inconsistent synthesis of Arminianism and
           Calvinism."[41]
        b. An Amyralidian view orders God's decrees as follows:
          01. Decree to appoint all mankind to eternal life, provided that
              they believe.
          02. Decree to send His Son to die for all men to implement this
              purpose.
          03. Decree to give special measure of the Spirit to some only
              (the elect), since some would not take initiative to repent
              and believe.


     7. How does God execute his decrees?
        a. "God executeth his decrees in the works of creation and
           providence." (WSC 8)
          01. Ps. 148:8; Isa. 40:26; Dan. 4:35; Acts 4:24-28; Rev. 4:11


     8. What is God's work of creation?
        a. "The work of creation is, God's making all things of nothing, by
           the word of his power, in the space of six days, and all very
           good." (WSC 9)


     9. Briefly discuss the length of the days of creation.
        a. See chart below.


    10. Evaluate biblically the theory of evolution.
        a. The definition of evolution is "genotypic change in frequency in
           a species over time." While such small scale genotypic change,
           or micro-evolution, is undoubtedly a fact (e.g., white moths can
           be change to peppered moths) it is scientifically suspect to
           extrapolate from this fact the theory of macro-evolution, that
           the diversity of life forms today can be explicable solely in
           terms of genotypic change in a species.
        b. On biblical grounds, evolutionary theory conflicts with the
           teaching of Genesis 2-3, reiterated in Romans 5, that death came
           from Adam's first sin. Moreover, theistic evolution reduces the
           personal God of the Bible to a deistic god. Natural selection is
           by definition a purposeless random process. The God of the Bible
           is not portrayed as setting the world up only to be ruled by a
           purposeless random process or even to be guided from afar by a
           divinely superintended process. Rather, God is portrayed as
           intimately involved in his creation (Gen 2-3).


    11. What [are] God's work[s] of providence?
        a. "God's works of providence are, his most holy, wise, and
           powerful preserving and governing all his creatures, and all
           their actions." (WSC 11)


    12. What is a miracle?
        a. WCF V.3: "God, in His ordinary providence, maketh use of means,
           yet is free to work without, above, and against them, at His
           pleasure."
        b. Quoting McPherson, Berkhof says that "A miracle is something
           done without recourse to the ordinary means of production, a
           result called forth directly by the first cause without the
           mediation, at least in the usual way, of second causes."[42] In
           other words, a miracle occurs from God's immediate causal
           intervention rather than his typical mediate governing of the
           universe. Or simply put, a miracle is a supernatural work of
           God.


    13. Do miracles occur today. Explain.
        a. WCF V.3: "God, in His ordinary providence, maketh use of means,
           yet is free to work without, above, and against them, at His
           pleasure."
        b. While God typically works through secondary causes, it is not
           beyond Him to work outside of them, even today. Though it is
           dangerous to assume the existence of "miracle workers" today (as
           were the apostles whose miracles validated their apostolic
           authority), certain miracles, such as miraculous healings,
           undoubtedly take place. These miracles, however, are not the
           norm.


    14. Is God responsible for sin?
        a. Did he decree it?
          01. WCF III.1: "God from all eternity, did, by the most wise and
              holy counsel of His own will, freely, and unchangeably ordain
              whatsoever comes to pass: yet so, as thereby neither is God
              the author of sin, nor is violence offered to the will of the
              creatures; nor is the liberty or contingency of second causes
              taken away, but rather established."
          02. WCF V.4: "The almighty power, unsearchable wisdom, and
              infinite goodness of God so far manifest themselves in His
              providence, that it extendeth itself even to the first fall,
              and all other sins of angels and men; and that not by a bare
              permission, but such as hath joined with it a most wise and
              powerful bounding, and otherwise ordering, and governing of
              them, in a manifold dispensation, to His own holy ends; yet
              so, as the sinfulness thereof proceedeth only from the
              creature, and not from God, who, being most holy and
              righteous, neither is nor can be the author or approver of
              sin."
          03. "God's eternal decree certainly rendered the entrance of sin
              into the world certain, but this may not be interpreted so as
              to make God the cause of sin in the sense of being its
              responsible author."[43]
        b. Did he permit it?
          01. WCF V.4: "The almighty power, unsearchable wisdom, and
              infinite goodness of God so far manifest themselves in His
              providence, that it extendeth itself even to the first fall,
              and all other sins of angels and men; and that not by a bare
              permission, but such as hath joined with it a most wise and
              powerful bounding, and otherwise ordering, and governing of
              them, in a manifold dispensation, to His own holy ends; yet
              so, as the sinfulness thereof proceedeth only from the
              creature, and not from God, who, being most holy and
              righteous, neither is nor can be the author or approver of
              sin."
          02. Since all things happen according to the counsel of God's
              will (Eph 1:11), it would have to be the case that God
              permitted sin.
        c. Who is the ultimate cause of all things?
          01. WCF V.2: "Although, in relation to the foreknowledge and
              decree of God, the first Cause, all things come to pass
              immutably, and infallibly; yet, by the same providence, He
              ordereth them to fall out, according to the nature of second
              causes, either necessarily, freely, or contingently."
          02. God is the ultimate cause of all things. However, God's
              causal relationship to good and evil is asymmetrical. In
              other words, God is responsible for good and is to be praised
              for it but he is never culpable for evil in a blameworthy
              way. "Dear friend, do not imitate what is evil but what is
              good. Anyone who does what is good is from God. Anyone who
              does what is evil has not seen God (3 John 11)." Notably,
              Calvin said that when we try to make God the author of sin we
              replicate Adam's implicit charge when he claimed that he ate
              from the tree because the woman whom God had made gave him
              the fruit (Gen 3:12).
        d. What is theodicy?
          01. A theodicy attempts to explain how all of the following
              propositions can be true and yet God is not culpable for
              evil.
              i. God exists.
             ii. God is omnipotent.
            iii. God is omniscient.
             iv. God is omnibenevolent.
              v. God created the world.
             vi. Evil exists.


    15. If God is good and all-powerful, explain AIDS, deformed babies,
        natural disasters.
        a. The Bible makes it clear that the world is fallen due to Adam's
           sin and because of that tragedies afflict even innocent people.
           The book of Job teaches us to have faith in God the giver above
           his gifts even when we are afflicted.




    16. Discuss the biblical teaching regarding predestination, election,
        and reprobation.
        a. WCF III.3-8:
          01. WCF III.3: "By the decree of God, for the manifestation of
              His glory, some men and angels are predestinated unto
              everlasting life; and others foreordained to everlasting
              death."
          02. WCF III.4: "These angels and men, thus predestinated, and
              foreordained, are particularly and unchangeably designed, and
              their number so certain and definite, that it cannot be
              either increased or diminished."
          03. WCF III.5: "Those of mankind that are predestinated unto
              life, God, before the foundation of the world was laid,
              according to His eternal and immutable purpose, and the
              secret counsel and good pleasure of His will, hath chosen, in
              Christ, unto everlasting glory, out of His mere free grace
              and love, without any foresight of faith, or good works, or
              perseverance in either of them, or any other thing in the
              creature, as conditions, or causes moving Him thereunto: and
              all to the praise of His glorious grace."
          04. WCF III.6: "As God hath appointed the elect unto glory, so
              hath He, by the eternal and most free purpose of His will,
              foreordained all the mans thereunto. Wherefore, they who are
              elected, being fallen in Adam, are redeemed by Christ, are
              effectually called unto faith in Christ by His Spirit working
              in due season, are justified,adopted, sanctified, and kept by
              His power, through faith, unto salvation. Neither are any
              other redeemed by Christ, effectually called, justified,
              adopted, sanctified, and saved, but the elect only."
          05. WCF III.7: "The rest of mankind God was pleased, according to
              the unsearchable counsel of His own will, whereby He
              extendeth or witholdeth mercy, as He pleaseth, for the glory
              of His sovereign power over His creatures, to pass by; and to
              ordain them to dishonour and wrath for their sin, to the
              praise of His glorious justice."
          06. WCF III.8: "The doctrine of this high mystery of
              predestination is to be handled with special prudence and
              care, that men, attending the will of God revealed in His
              Word, and yielding obedience thereunto, may, from the
              certainty of their effectual vocation, be assured of their
              eternal election. So shall this doctrine afford matter of
              praise, reverence, and admiration of God; and of humility,
              diligence, and abundant consolation to all that sincerely
              obey the Gospel."
        b. Predestination
          01. "Predestination includes two parts, namely, election and
              reprobation, the predetermination of both the good and the
              wicked to their final end, and to certain proximate ends
              which are instrumental in the realization of their final
              destiny."[44]
        c. Election
          01. Though the Bible speaks of election in various ways, such as
              the election of Israel (Deut 7:6), when it comes salvation,
              election may be defined as "that eternal act of God whereby
              He, in His sovereign good pleasure, and on account of no
              foreseen merit in them, chooses a certain number of men to be
              the recipients of special grace and of eternal
              salvation."[45] (Eph 1:3-4)
          02. The proximate purpose of salvation is the salvation of the
              elect but its final aim is the glory of God.
        d. Reprobation
          01. Reprobation is "that eternal decree of God whereby He has
              determined to pass some men by with the operations of His
              special grace, and to punish them for their sins, to the
              manifestation of His justice."[46]
          02. Scriptural Proofs:
              i. 1 Peter 2:8:
                  . "...They stumble because they disobey the message--
                    which is also what they were destined for."
             ii. Jude 4:
                  . "For certain men whose condemnation was written about
                    long ago have secretly slipped in among you. They are
                    godless men, who change the grace of our God into a
                    license for immorality and deny Jesus Christ our only
                    Sovereign and Lord."


    17. Distinguish the Reformed and Arminian understandings of these
        doctrines.
        a. Arminians do not accept the Reformed doctrine of absolute
           predestination. Rather, they support the doctrine of conditional
           predestination, that is, that God predestined His "elect" based
           conditionally upon His foreknowledge that they would believe.
           They incorrectly interpret foreknowledge as pertaining only to
           one's choice of Christ rather than to one's person, often citing
           1 Peter 1:2, which says that the elect were chosen according to
           foreknowledge. Such a view, however, puts salvation ultimately
           into the hands of man.


    18. Are these doctrines compatible with belief in real human choice and
        responsibility?
        a. WCF III.1: "God from all eternity, did, by the most wise and
           holy counsel of His own will, freely, and unchangeably ordain
           whatsoever comes to pass: yet so, as thereby neither is God the
           author of sin, nor is violence offered to the will of the
           creatures; nor is the liberty or contingency of second causes
           taken away, but rather established."
        b. Yes. Biblical teaching is compatibilistic in that it affirms the
           compatibility of God's sovereignty with human choice and
           responsibility. As Genesis 50:20 ("You intended to harm me, but
           God intended it for good to accomplish what is now being done,
           the saving of many lives") alludes to, God stands sovereignly
           behind even our choices and yet we are responsible for them (cf.
           Acts 2:23: "This man was handed over to you by God's set purpose
           and foreknowledge; and you, with the help of wicked men, put him
           to death by nailing him to the cross").




    19. What should be some of the practical results of believing the
        doctrine of election?
        a. WCF III.8: "The doctrine of this high mystery of predestination
           is to be handled with special prudence and care, that men,
           attending the will of God revealed in His Word, and yielding
           obedience thereunto, may, from the certainty of their effectual
           vocation, be assured of their eternal election. So shall this
           doctrine afford matter of praise, reverence, and admiration of
           God; and of humility, diligence, and abundant consolation to all
           that sincerely obey the Gospel."
        b. Practical results include:
          01. Gratitude for God's gracious, unmerited adoption.
          02. Reassurance that salvation does not depend upon us but upon
              God and thus is secure.
          03. Evangelistic incentive because the Lord has other sheep who
              must be brought in to the fold (John 10:16).




    20. Is there a real, personal devil? Explain from Scripture.
        a. Yes. The temptation accounts, for example, certainly portray the
           devil as real and personal (e.g. Mat 4:1-11).


   C. Humankind (WCF 4, 6, 9; WLC 14-17, 21-29; WSC 8-10, 13-19)


     1. What is the chief end of man?
        a. "Man's chief end is to glorify God, and to enjoy him for ever."
           (WSC 1)
          01. 1 Cor 10:31: "...do all to the glory of God."


     2. What is the supreme activity of man? (Catechism: "What does God
        require of man?")
        a. "The duty which God requireth of man, is obedience to his
           revealed will." (WSC 39)


     3. What does the Bible teach about the creation of human beings?
        a. "God created man male and female, after his own image, in
           knowledge, righteousness, and holiness, with dominion over the
           creatures." (WSC 10)


     4. In what way is man created in the image of God?
        a. In short, mankind is created functionally (love God, love
           neighbor, love creation) and structurally (endowments like
           rationality, spirituality, morality) in the image of God.
        b. According to Genesis 1, God created man, or humankind, alone in
           his image (Gen 1:26, 27). Thus the essence of humanity, what
           differentiates humankind from the rest of creation, is the imago
           dei. This imago dei appears to be both structural and
           functional.[47] The functional aspect is particularly prominent
           in Genesis, and seems to include humankind's relationship with
           creation, each other, and God. Humankind's relationship with
           creation is seen in the association of the imago dei with man's
           function as ruler over creation (Gen 1:26). The fact that
           humankind, both male and female, was created in God's image (Gen
           1:27) and is called to populate the earth suggests that
           functioning properly in human relationships is part of the imago
           dei. As for man's relationship with God, though only assumed in
           Genesis 1, it is central to Genesis 2 and 3 and thus forms the
           third aspect of what it means for humankind to image God.
           Finally, underlying the functional aspect of the imago dei is
           the structural one. If man is to function as God's unique image-
           bearer then it seems quite natural to assume he possesses
           certain special endowments or capacities, such as rationality,
           morality, and spirituality, in accordance with the imago dei.
           These endowments enable him to perform his threefold function as
           God's image-bearer.[48]
        c. Yet, rather than men and women imaging God as they should, the
           fall of humankind, depicted in Genesis 3, led to the corruption,
           though not the annihilation, of the imago dei. The image of God
           was not annihilated in the fall, one presumes, because humankind
           continues to possess to the unique structural attributes which
           form part of the imago dei.[49] Thus humankind is still said to
           bear the image of God, even after the fall (Gen 9:6). However,
           when one examines the effect of the fall on humankind's
           relationship with God, one another, and creation, it is clear
           that the fall radically altered the functional aspect of the
           imago dei.[50] Before the fall, humankind appeared to relate
           easily to God as well as to have unfettered access to him in the
           Garden (e.g., Gen 2:15-16). After the fall, however, humankind
           related to God with difficulty, hiding from him (Gen 3:8), and
           then losing access to God when Adam and the woman were thrown
           out of the Garden (Gen 3:23). With regard to humankind's
           relationship with one another, before the fall Adam and the
           woman were apparently united in perfect intimacy (Gen 2:25), but
           as a consequence of the fall their relationship became afflicted
           with conflict (Gen 3:16). As for humankind's relationship with
           creation, God's judgment reveals that it also would be marked by
           conflict due to the fall (Gen 3:17-19).
        d. An important implication of this understanding of the imago dei
           is that fallen human beings, in relating to God, one another,
           and creation, fail to reflect properly the image of God. Thus
           all people, whether civilized or uncivilized, are less than
           fully human. The only exception to this rule is the God-man
           Jesus Christ. According to Colossians 1:15, he is the image of
           the invisible God. Consequently, in his incarnation Jesus not
           only reveals God to us (John 1:18), but as the second Adam (1
           Cor 15:45) and the true Israelite (John 1:47) Jesus also reveals
           true humanity. When we examine the gospels we see that Jesus
           relates perfectly to God, his fellow humans, and creation. Jesus
           exercises dominion over creation (Mark 4.35). He loves God as
           the obedient son by doing everything the Father instructs him
           (John 5) and Jesus' love for his neighbor is evident everywhere
           (e.g. John 11:5, 36).


     5. Are all men in the image of God? If so, to what extent?
        a. All men are, indeed, created in the image of God (Gen 1:27; 9:6;
           Jam 3:9). Yet, all men are fallen and thus the image of God in
           man is marred by sin. Thus though mankind retains the structural
           aspect of the imago dei, the functional aspect is affected to
           the extent that mankind fails to love God and his neighbor as he
           should.


     6. How does the doctrine of the image of God influence debates about
        gender roles?
        a. Genesis 1:27 teaches not only that men and women are equal in
           worth and dignity due to being having been created in the imago
           dei but also implies that full humanity is exemplified in the
           two together rather than one or the other. However, as Paul
           teaches in 1 Tim 2:8-15 the fact that Adam was created first
           (Gen 2) implies that he was to headship authority in the
           relationship.


     7. Where did man's soul come from? When?
        a. The WCF simply says of the first couple that God "created man,
           male and female, with reasonable and immortal souls..." (IV.2).
           It thus does not address the way the soul was transmitted
           thereafter. Traditionally there are three positions:
          01. Pre-existence: Men's souls existed in some previous state.
          02. Traducianism: The souls of men are propagated along with
              their bodies at generation, and are therefore transmitted to
              children by parents.
          03. Creationism: Individual souls are immediate creations of God,
              owing its origin to a direct creative act at a time which
              cannot be precisely determined.
        b. Evaluation: Pre-existence lacks exegetical support and is
           theologically and philosophically objectionable. With regard to
           Traducianism and Creationism, supporters can be found for both
           views. Buswell supports Traducianism while Berkhof argues for
           Creationism. Arguments for these two views are as follows:
          01. Traducianism:
              i. Genesis 1:27 and 2:7 suggests that God breathed life into
                 man but once, and then left him to propagate.
             ii. Nothing is said about the creation of Eve's soul.
            iii. God has ceased his work of creation.
             iv. Descendants are often spoken of as "being in the loins of
                 their father" (e.g. Heb 7:9-10).
          02. Creationism:
              i. The creation account points to a distinction between body
                 and soul.
             ii. Hebrews 12:9 refers to God as the Father of our spirits.
            iii. This position avoids the Christological problem of Christ's
                 soul possibly sharing in Adam's sin.


     8. What is sin? (See WSC 14) Include Scripture proofs.
        a. "Sin is any want of conformity unto, or transgression of, the
           law of God" (WSC 14).
          01. Romans 3:23: "for all have sinned and fall short of the glory
              of God."
          02. 1 John 3:4: "Everyone who sins breaks the law; in fact, sin
              is lawlessness."


     9. Discuss the guilt of sin and the corruption of sin.
        a. The WSC 18 says "The sinfulness of that estate whereinto man
           fell, consists in the guilt of Adam's first sin, the want of
           original righteousness, and the corruption of his whole nature,
           which is commonly called Original Sin; together with all actual
           transgressions which proceed from it."
        b. Inherited Guilt:
          01. Romans 5:19: "For just as through the disobedience of the one
              man the many were made sinners, so also through the obedience
              of the one man the many will be made righteous."
          02. Hebrews 7:9-10: "One might even say that Levi, who collects
              the tenth, paid the tenth through Abraham, 10 because when
              Melchizedek met Abraham, Levi was still in the body of his
              ancestor."
        c. Inherited Corruption:
          01. Romans 3:23


    10. Define and discuss original sin (include Scripture proofs)
        a. WSC 16: "The covenant being made with Adam, not only for
           himself, but for his posterity; all mankind, descending from him
           by ordinary generation, sinned in him, and fell with him, in his
           first transgression."
        b. WSC 17: "The fall brought mankind into an estate of sin and
           misery."
        c. WSC 18: "The sinfulness of that estate whereinto man fell,
           consists in the guilt of Adam's first sin, the want of original
           righteousness, and the corruption of his whole nature, which is
           commonly called Original Sin; together with all actual
           transgressions which proceed from it."
        d. Scriptural Proofs:
          01. Romans 5:12-21


    11. How can original sin be reconciled to the canons of justice either
        human or divine?
        a. Total depravity and total inability do not relieve man of moral
           obligation, because man's inability is self-imposed, grounded in
           Adam's sin, and not due to any limitation which God put upon
           man.


    12. Is there any good remaining in a fallen, sinful man? Explain.
        a. WCF VI.4: "From this original corruption, whereby we are utterly
           indisposed, disabled, and made opposite to all good, and wholly
           inclined to all evil, do proceed all actual transgressions."
          01. Isaiah 64:6: "All of us have become like one who is unclean,
              and all our righteous acts are like filthy rags; we all
              shrivel up like a leaf, and like the wind our sins sweep us
              away."
        b. WCF IX.3 suggests that fallen man's opposition to good is
           specifically in relation to salvation: "Man, by his fall into a
           state of sin, hath wholly lost all ability of will to any
           spiritual good accompanying salvation: so as, a natural man,
           being altogether averse from that good, and dead in sin, is not
           able, by his own strength, to convert himself, or to prepare
           himself thereunto."
        c. Yet, because fallen man continues to be made in the image of God
           there must be some good in him (Gen 9:6; James 3:9).


    13. In what ways does man sin?
        a. WSC 14: "Sin is any want of conformity unto, or transgression
           of, the law of God."


    14. What are some of the consequences of sin?
        a. WSC 18: "The sinfulness of that estate whereinto man fell,
           consists in the guilt of Adam's first sin, the want of original
           righteousness, and the corruption of his whole nature, which is
           commonly called Original Sin; together with all actual
           transgressions which proceed from it."
        b. WSC 19: "All mankind by their fall lost communion with God, are
           under his wrath and curse, and so made liable to all miseries in
           this life, to death itself, and to the pains of hell for ever."


    15. Is the will of man free? Explain (include Scripture proofs).


        a. Was Adam's will (before he sinned) free? Explain (include
           Scripture proofs).
          01. WCF IX.1: "God hath endued the will of man with that natural
              liberty, that it is neither forced, nor, by any absolute
              necessity of nature, determined to good, or evil."
          02. WCF IX.2: "Man, in his state of innocency, had freedom, and
              power to will and to do that which was good and well pleasing
              to God; but yet, mutably, so that he might fall from it."


        b. Can a sinner do anything good? Explain (include Scripture
           proofs).
          01. WCF IX.3: "Man, by his fall into a state of sin, hath wholly
              lost all ability of will to any spiritual good accompanying
              salvation: so as, a natural man, being altogether averse from
              that good, and dead in sin, is not able, by his own strength,
              to convert himself, or to prepare himself thereunto."
          02. Isaiah 64:6: "All of us have become like one who is unclean,
              and all our righteous acts are like filthy rags; we all
              shrivel up like a leaf, and like the wind our sins sweep us
              away."


        c. Is a sinner's will free to believe? Explain (include Scripture
           proofs).
          01. WCF IX.3: "Man, by his fall into a state of sin, hath wholly
              lost all ability of will to any spiritual good accompanying
              salvation: so as, a natural man, being altogether averse from
              that good, and dead in sin, is not able, by his own strength,
              to convert himself, or to prepare himself thereunto."
          02. Ephesians 2:8-10: "For it is by grace you have been saved,
              through faith-- and this not from yourselves, it is the gift
              of God-- 9 not by works, so that no one can boast. 10 For we
              are God's workmanship, created in Christ Jesus to do good
              works, which God prepared in advance for us to do."


        d. Does a believer have free will? Explain (include Scripture
           proofs).
          01. WCF IX.4: "When God converts a sinner, and translates him
              into the state of grace, He freeth him from his natural
              bondage under sin; and, by His grace alone, enables him
              freely to will and to do that which is spiritually good; yet
              so, that by reason of his remaining corruption, he doth not
              perfectly, nor only, will that which is good, but doth also
              will that which is evil."
          02. Ephesians 2:8-10: see above
          03. Philippians 2:12-13: "...continue to work out your salvation
              with fear and trembling, 13 for it is God who works in you to
              will and to act according to his good purpose."


   D. God's Way of Salvation (WCF 7-8; WLC 20, 30-56; WSC 12, 20-28)


     1. What is a covenant?
        a. "A covenant is a pact or agreement between two parties. It may
           be, and among men most generally is, an agreement [between equal
           parties]; but it may also be [imposed by a superior upon an
           inferior]. It is generally confirmed by a solemn ceremony as in
           the presence of God, and thereby obtains an inviolable
           character. Each one of the parties binds himself to the
           fulfilment of certain promises on the basis of stipulated
           conditions."[51]


     2. What is the Covenant of Works (Life)? Who are its parties?
        a. WCF VII.2: "The first covenant made with man was a covenant of
           works, wherein life was promised to Adam; and in him to his
           posterity, upon condition of perfect and personal obedience."
        b. WSC 12: "When God had created man, he entered into a covenant of
           life with him, upon condition of perfect obedience; forbidding
           him to eat of the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, upon
           the pain of death."


     3. Is there a present validity to the Covenant of Works (Life)? What
        is it?
        a. Yes and no. Christ has fulfilled it so that we don't have to.
        b. "Though the covenant of redemption is the eternal basis of the
           covenant of grace, and, as far as sinners are concerned, also
           its eternal prototype, it was for Christ a covenant of works
           rather than a covenant of grace. For Him the law of the original
           covenant applied, namely, that eternal life could only be
           obtained by meeting the demands of the law. As the last Adam
           Christ obtains eternal life for sinners in reward for faithful
           obedience, and not at all as an unmerited gift of grace. And
           what He has done as the Representative and Surety of all His
           people, they are no more in duty bound to do. The work has been
           done, the reward is merited, and believers are made partakers of
           the fruits of Christ's accomplished work through grace."[52]


     4. What is the Covenant of Redemption? Who are its parties?
        a. "The covenant of redemption may be defined as the agreement
           between the Father, giving the Son as Head and Redeemer of the
           elect, and the Son, voluntarily taking the place of those whom
           the Father had given Him."[53]
        b. John 10:14-18:
          01. "'I am the good shepherd; I know my sheep and my sheep know
              me-15 just as the Father knows me and I know the Father-and I
              lay down my life for the sheep. 16 I have other sheep that
              are not of this sheep pen. I must bring them also. They too
              will listen to my voice, and there shall be one flock and one
              shepherd. 17 The reason my Father loves me is that I lay down
              my life-- only to take it up again. 18 No one takes it from
              me, but I lay it down of my own accord. I have authority to
              lay it down and authority to take it up again. This command I
              received from my Father.'"


     5. Discuss the Covenant of Grace.
        a. Westminster Standards
          01. WCF VII.3: "Man, by his fall, having made himself incapable
              of life by that covenant, the Lord was pleased to make a
              second, commonly called the covenant of grace; wherein He
              freely offereth unto sinners life and salvation by Jesus
              Christ; requiring of them faith in Him, that they may be
              saved, and promising to give unto all those that are ordained
              unto eternal life His Holy Spirit, to make them willing, and
              able to believe."
          02. WCF VII.4: "This covenant of grace is frequently set forth in
              Scripture by the name of a testament, in reference to the
              death of Jesus Christ the Testator, and to the everlasting
              inheritance, with all things belonging to it, therein
              bequeathed."
          03. WCF VII.5: "This covenant was differently administered in the
              time of the law, and in the time of the gospel: under the law
              it was administered by promises, prophecies, sacrifices,
              circumcision, the paschal lamb, and other types and
              ordinances delivered to the people of the Jews, all
              foresignifying Christ to come; which were, for that time,
              sufficient and efficacious, through the operation of the
              Spirit, to instruct and build up the elect in faith in the
              promised Messiah, by whom they had full remission of sins,
              and eternal salvation; and is called the old Testament."
          04. WCF VII 6: "Under the gospel, when Christ, the substance, was
              exhibited, the ordinances in which this covenant is dispensed
              are the preaching of the Word, and the administration of the
              sacraments of Baptism and the Lord's Supper: which, though
              fewer in number, and administered with more simplicity, and
              less outward glory, yet, in them, it is held forth in more
              fullness, evidence, and spiritual efficacy, to all nations,
              both Jews and Gentiles; and is called the new Testament.
              There are not therefore two covenants of grace, differing in
              substance, but one and the same, under various
              dispensations."
          05. WSC 20: "God having, out of his mere good pleasure, from all
              eternity, elected some to everlasting life, did enter into a
              covenant of grace, to deliver them out of the estate of sin
              and misery, and to bring them into an estate of salvation by
              a Redeemer."
        b. Berkhof
          01. Definiton:
              i. "The covenant of grace may be defined as that gracious
                 agreement between the offended God and the offending but
                 elect sinner, in which God promises salvation through faith
                 in Christ, and the sinner accepts this believingly,
                 promising a life of faith and obedience."[54]
        c. Contents of the Covenant of Grace
          01. The Promises of God
              i. "The main promise of God, which includes all other
                 promises, is contained in the oft-repeated words, 'I will
                 be a God unto thee, and to thy seed after thee.' [cf. Gen
                 17:7; Lev 26:12]...This one promise really includes all
                 other promises, such as...."[55]
                  . Promise of various temporal blessings, which often serve
                    to symbolize the spiritual kind.
                  . Promise of justification, including adoption of children
                    and a claim to life eternal
                  . The Promise of the Spirit of God for the application,
                    full and free, of the work of redemption and of all the
                    blessings of salvation
                  . The promise of final glorification in a life that never
                    ends
          02. The Response of Man
              i. "In general, the relation between the covenant God and the
                 single believer or believers collectively is represented as
                 the close relationship between man and wife, bridegroom and
                 bride, a father and his children. This implies that the
                 response of those who share the covenant blessings will be
                 one of true, faithful, trustful, consecrated, and devoted
                 love."[56]
             ii. "To the general promise, 'I will be thy God,' man responds
                 by saying, 'I will belong to thy people,' and by casting
                 his lot with the people of God."[57]
            iii. "And to the promise of justification unto the forgiveness
                 of sins, the adoption of children, and eternal life, he
                 responds by saving faith in Jesus Christ, by trust in Him
                 for time and eternity, and by a life of obedience and
                 consecration to God."[58]
        d. Characteristics of the Covenant of Grace
          01. Gracious Covenant
          02. Trinitarian Covenant
          03. Eternal and therefore Unbreakable Covenant (Heb 13:20)
          04. Particular and not Universal
          05. It is essentially the same in all dispensations, though its
              form of administration changes.


     6. How is the Covenant of Grace related to:


        a. The Abrahamic Covenant?[59]
          01. "Up to the time of Abraham there was no formal establishment
              of the covenant of grace. While Gen. 3:15 already contains
              the elements of this covenant, it does not record a formal
              transaction by which the covenant was established. It does
              not even speak explicitly of a covenant. The establishment of
              the covenant with Abraham marked the beginning of an
              institutional Church."
          02. "In the transaction with Abraham the particularistic Old
              Testament administration of the covenant had its beginning,
              and it becomes perfectly evident that man is a party in the
              covenant and must respond to the promises of God by faith."
          03. "The spiritual blessings of the covenant of grace become fare
              more apparent in the covenant with Abraham than they were
              before." (cf. Rom 4)
          04. "The covenant with Abraham already included a symbolical
              element...These temporal blessings did not constitute an end
              in themselves, but served to symbolize and typify spiritual
              and heavenly things."
          05.  "If there is a representative head in the covenant of grace,
              it can only be Christ...but, strictly speaking, we can
              consider Him as the Head only on the assumption that the
              covenant of redemption and the covenant of grace are one.
              Abraham can be called the head of the covenant only in the
              sense that it was formally established with him, and that he
              received the promise of its continuance in the line of his
              natural, but above all, of his spiritual, descendants."
          06. "Finally, we must not lose sight of the fact that the stage
              of the Old Testament covenant revelation which is most
              normative for us in the New Testament dispensation, is not
              that of the Sinaitic covenant, but that of the covenant
              established with Abraham. The Sinaitic covenant is an
              interlude, covering a period in which the real character of
              the covenant of grace, that is, its free and gracious
              character, is somewhat eclipsed by all kinds of external
              ceremonies and forms which, in connection with the theocratic
              life of Israel, placed the demands of the law prominently in
              the foreground, cf. Gal. 3. In the covenant with Abraham, on
              the other hand, the promise and the faith that responds to
              the promise are made emphatic."


        b. The Mosaic Covenant?[60]
          01. "The covenant of Sinai was essentially the same as that
              established with Abraham, though the form differed somewhat."
          02. "At Sinai the covenant became a truly national covenant."
          03. "The Sinaitic covenant included a service that contained a
              positive reminder of the strict demands of the covenant of
              works. The law was placed very much in the foreground, giving
              prominence once more to the earlier legal element. But the
              covenant of Sinai was not a renewal of the covenant of works;
              in it the law was made subservient to the covenant of
              grace...The law served a twofold purpose in connection with
              the covenant of grace: (1) to increase the consciousness of
              sin, Rom. 3:20; 4:15; Gal. 3:19; and (2) to be a tutor unto
              Christ, Gal. 3:24." [for law as rule of life see 05 below]
          04. "The covenant with the nation of Israel included a detailed
              ceremonial and typical service...A separate priesthood was
              instituted, and a continuous preaching of the gospel in
              symbols and types was introduced. These symbols and types
              appear under two different aspects: as the demands of God
              imposed on the people; and as a divine message of salvation
              to the people. The Jews lost sight of the latter aspect, and
              fixed their attention exclusively on the former. They
              regarded the covenant ever increasingly, but mistakenly, as a
              covenant of works, and saw in the symbols and types a mere
              appendage to this."
          05. "The law in the Sinaitic covenant also served Israel as a
              rule of life, so that the one law of God assumed three
              different aspects, designated as the moral, civil, and the
              ceremonial or religious law."

        c. The Noahic Covenant?[61]
          01. "It is a covenant conferring only natural blessings, and is
              therefore often called the covenant of nature or of common
              grace."
          02. Points of Differences:
              i. "While the covenant of grace pertains primarily, though not
                 exclusively, to spiritual blessings, the covenant of nature
                 assures man only of earthly and temporal blessings."
             ii. "While the covenant of grace in the broadest sense of the
                 word includes only believers and their seed, and is fully
                 realized only in the lives of the elect, the covenant with
                 Noah was not only universal in it inception, but was
                 destined to remain all-inclusive."
          03. Points of Connection:
              i. "The covenant of nature also originated in the grace of
                 God...By nature man has no claim whatsoever on the natural
                 blessings promised in [the Noahic] covenant."
             ii. "This covenant also rests on the covenant of grace. It was
                 established more particularly with Noah and his seed,
                 because there were clear evidences of the realization of
                 the covenant of grace in this family, Gen. 6:9; 7:1; 9:9,
                 26, 27."
            iii. "It is also a necessary appendage...of the covenant of
                 grace."


     7. Discuss the relationship between the Old and New Covenants. Defend
        from Scripture.
        a. Short Answer:
          01. They are distinct (Jer 31:31-34; 2 Cor 3:3-6; Heb 8:8-9, 13;
              9:15), but they are the same in substance in that they
              contain God's promise that He will be the God of His chosen
              people (2 Cor 6:16-18; Heb 8:10; Rev 21:2-3) by redeeming
              them in Christ. Yet, the New Covenant is superior to the Old
              (Heb 7:20-22, 28; 8:6). They differ in the way that they are
              administered by God (Gal 3:23-25; 4:1-7). In this latter
              sense, it can properly be said that the New Covenant
              displaces the Old Covenant (Heb 8:13; 10:9).
        b. Berkhof[62]
          01. "The covenant of grace, as it is revealed in the New
              Testament, is essentially the same as that which governed the
              relation of Old Testament believers to God." (cf. Rom 4; Eph
              2:11-22; 3:6)
          02. "The New Testament dispensation differs from that of the Old
              in that it is universal, that is, extends to all nations."
              (cf. Mat 28:18-20)
          03. "The New Testament dispensation places greater emphasis on
              the gracious character of the covenant." (cf. 2 Cor 3)
          04. "Finally, the New Testament dispensation brings richer
              blessings than the Old Testament dispensation. The revelation
              of God's grace reached its climax, when the Word became flesh
              and dwelt among men 'full of grace and truth.' The Holy
              Spirit is poured out upon the Church, and out of the fulness
              of the grace of God in Christ enriches believers with
              spiritual and eternal blessings." (John 1:17-18)
        c. Calvin[63]
          01. Similarities
              i. "The covenant made with all the patriarchs is so much like
                 ours in substance and reality that the two are actually one
                 and the same. Yet they differ in the mode of dispensation."
          02. Differences
              i. The "Lord of old willed that his people direct and elevate
                 their minds to the heavenly heritage; yet, to nourish them
                 better in this hope, he displayed it for them to see and,
                 so to speak, taste, under earthly benefits. But now that
                 the gospel has more plainly and clearly revealed the grace
                 of the future life, the Lord leads our minds to meditate
                 upon it directly, laying aside the lower mode of training
                 that he used with the Israelites."
             ii. "The second difference between the Old and New Testaments
                 consists in figures: that, in the absence of the reality,
                 it showed but an image and shadow in the place of the
                 substance; the New Testament reveals the very substance of
                 truth as present." (cf. Heb 10:1)
            iii. "I come to the third difference, taken from Jeremiah
                 [quotes Jer 31:31-34] From these words the apostle took
                 occasion to make a comparison between the law and the
                 gospel, calling the former literal, the latter spiritual
                 doctrine; the former he speaks of as carved on tablets of
                 stone, the latter as written upon men's hearts; the former
                 is the preaching of death, the latter of life; the former
                 of condemnation, the latter of righteousness; the former to
                 be made void, the latter to abide [II Cor. 3:6-11]."
             iv. "The fourth difference arises out of the third. Scripture
                 calls the Old Testament one of 'bondage' because it
                 produces fear in men's minds; but the New Testament, one of
                 'freedom' because it lifts them to trust and assurance."
              v. "The fifth difference, which may be added, lies in the fact
                 that until the advent of Christ, the Lord set apart one
                 nation within which to confine the covenant of grace."


     8. Explain and defend (with Scripture proofs) the orthodox doctrine of
        the person of Christ.
        a. WCF VIII.2: "The Son of God, the second person in the Trinity,
           being very and eternal God, of one substance and equal with the
           Father, did, when the fulness of time was come, take upon Him
           man's nature, with all the essential properties, and common
           infirmities thereof, yet without sin; being conceived by the
           power of the Holy Ghost, in the womb of the virgin Mary, of her
           substance. So that two whole, perfect, and distinct natures, the
           Godhead and the manhood, were inseparably joined together in one
           person, without conversion, composition, or confusion. Which
           person is very God, and very man, yet one Christ, the only
           Mediator between God and man."
        b. WSC 21: "The only Redeemer of God's elect is the Lord Jesus
           Christ, who, being the eternal Son of God, became man and so
           was, and continueth to be, God and man in two distinct natures,
           and one person, forever."
        c. Jesus' Divinity & Humanity
          01. John 1:1, 14
          02. John 20:27-28
          03. Col 1:15, 19 (divinity); 1 Tim 2:5 (humanity)


     9. Briefly discuss the natures of Christ, including:


        a. Was Christ a human person?
          01. Yes in the sense that he had a human nature (John 1:1, 14).
              Better put, he was one person with two natures, that is, a
              human and divine nature.


        b. Does Christ have a soul?
          01. Yes. As Christ said, "...My soul is overwhelmed with sorrow
              to the point of death..." (Mat 26:38).


        c. What is kenosis?
          01. It means "self-emptying" and is the term used to discuss that
              of which Christ emptied himself.
          02. According to the WLC 46, Christ emptied himself of his glory.
              Specifically, it states: "The estate of Christ's humiliation
              was that low condition, wherein he for our sakes, emptying
              himself of his glory, took upon him the form of a servant, in
              his conception and birth, life, death, and after his death,
              until his resurrection."


        d. Did Christ lay aside any of His divine attributes at the
           incarnation?
          01. WCF VIII.3: "The Lord Jesus, in His human nature thus united
              to the divine, was sanctified, and anointed with the Holy
              Spirit, above measure, having in Him all the treasures of
              wisdom and knowledge; in whom it pleased the Father that all
              fulness should dwell; to the end that, being holy, harmless,
              undefiled, and full of grace and truth, He might be
              thoroughly furnished to execute the office of a Mediator and
              Surety...."
          02. No, as Colossians 2:9 says, "For in Christ all the fullness
              of the Deity lives in bodily form."
          03. Yet it would seem that Christ would have had to give up his
              attribute of omnipresence sense a body is necessarily local.
              This, of course, is what the controversy with the Lutherans
              is all about regarding the communicatio idiomaturm (the
              communication of Christ's attributes such that divine
              attributes can be posited of his human nature).
          04. Berkhof says that Christ gave up omniscience and omnipresence
              while Grudem denies this.


        e. Briefly explain and defend against the challenges to orthodox
           Christology posed by:


          01. Docetism
              i. A view that says, "Christ only seemed to become a man."
                 Jesus laid this view aside even in His resurrection by
                 inviting Thomas to touch His wounds (John 20:24ff.).


          02. Arius
              i. He believed that Jesus was a heavenly being, an
                 intermediary between God and man, but still a creature
                 himself. Arius denied the divinity of Christ because he
                 felt this would challenge the monotheistic beliefs that he
                 held. Arians argued that Christ was heteroousion rather
                 than homoousion. His view was condemned at Nicea in A.D.
                 325.


          03. Nestorius
              i. Nestorius wanted to so emphasize the distinction between
                 Christ's human nature and his divine nature that his
                 opponents charged him with creating two persons. This was
                 the argument of Cyril-that Nestorius split the God-man into
                 two persons, who one moment acts as God (doing miracles,
                 etc.) but the next moment becomes the person of man (who
                 gets tired, etc.).
             ii. Nestorius' viewpoint was condemned at the Council of
                 Ephesus in 431 and Nestorius spent the rest of his life in
                 exile


          04. Apollinarius
              i. Apollinarius believed that Jesus was a human being like we
                 are but he did not have a human mind/soul but has a divine
                 Logos combined with the man Christ. Thus Christ had a body
                 like we have but not a soul like we have. Jesus was divine
                 soul/mind united with human flesh.
             ii. Apolliniariansm is condemned at the Council of
                 Constantinople in 381. This view does not explain how
                 Christ could have been tempted.


          05. Eutyches
              i. Similar to Apollinarius, Eutyches thought Christ had two
                 natures but that the divine being was so vast that Christ's
                 humanity was of little effect, like a drop of honey in the
                 ocean.
             ii. This view is sometimes called monophysitism because it, in
                 effect, teaches that Christ had only one nature.


        f. Are any of the early heresies regarding the natures of Christ
           held today? If so, by whom?
          01. The Coptic church is monphysite. Also, the Mormons' belief
              that "As man is God once was and as God is man will become"
              includes definite heresy with regard to Jesus' person.


        g. Explain and defend the doctrine of the "communication of
           properties."
          01. "A communicatio idiomatum, or communication of properties.
              This means that the properties of both, the human and the
              divine natures, are now the properties of the person, and are
              therefore ascribed to the person. The person can be said to
              be almighty, omniscient, omnipresent, and so on, but can also
              be called a man of sorrows, of limited knowledge and power,
              and subject to human want and miseries. We must be careful
              not to understand the term to mean that anything peculiar to
              the divine nature was communicated to the human nature, or
              vice versa; or that there is an interpenetration of the two
              natures, as a result of which the divine is humanized, and
              the human is deified (Rome). The deity cannot share in human
              weakness; neither can man participate in any of the essential
              perfections of the Godhead."[64]


        h. What is the "extra Calvinisticum?"


          01. It is the view that the deity of the Son of God was not
              completely exhausted in the God-man revelation such that the
              Son of God has an existence that was also beyond the flesh
              i. See Institutes Book II, chapter xiii, paragraph 4: "They
                 thrust upon us as something absurd the fact that if the
                 Word of God became flesh, then he was confined within the
                 narrow prison of an earthly body. This is mere impudence!
                 For even if the Word in his immeasurable essence united
                 with the nature of man into one person, we do not imagine
                 that he was confined therein. Here is something marvelous:
                 the Son of God descended from heaven in such a way that,
                 without leaving heaven, he willed to be borne in the
                 virgin's womb, to go about the earth, and to hang upon the
                 cross; yet he continuously filled the world even as he had
                 done from the beginning!"
                  . Calvin doesn't go into any further detail; he recognizes
                    that he is flirting with speculation. We see at work in
                    the above paragraph Calvin's conceptual principle of
                    finitum est non capax infinit, that is, "the finite
                    cannot contain the infinite."
                  . Calvin is not saying that Jesus was not fully incarnate
                    or was only partially incarnate
                  . Calvin's point was that deity was not fully absorbed by
                    humanity; the Son of God continued to rule heaven and
                    earth and sustain all of creation even as the God- man
                    walked on earth
          02. Lutherans charge Calvin with Nestorianism though he rejects
              this charge.
          03. This view is not as distinct as it is sometimes thought to be
              since it was also held by many prominent thinkers in the
              Medieval church, e.g. Aquinas
          04. Why did Calvin argue this?
              i. He thought it was biblical and that it protected the full
                 deity of the Son. In Jesus we are not dealing with humanity
                 raised to the Nth degree
          05. This principle is given expression in the Heidelberg
              Catechism (1563)
              i. "The divinity [of the Son of God] was beyond the bounds of
                 the humanity and is nonetheless in that humanity and
                 related to it"


        i. Define the following names:


          01. Jesus
              i. VIhsouj coming from the Greek form of the Hebrew "Joshua"
                 meaning "Yahweh is salvation."


          02. Son of Man
              i. "Son of Man" was Jesus' favorite self-designation. Its
                 background is Daniel 7:14 (cf. Mat 26:64) and it thus
                 alludes to Jesus' divinity.


          03. Christ
              i. Cri,stoj is the NT form for the OT "Messiah" meaning "the
                 anointed one."


          04. Son of God
              i. Two sense are meant here. Christ is the Son of God in that
                 he is the second person of the Trinity, and he is the Son
                 of God in that he owes his birth to the supernatural
                 activity of the Holy Spirit.


          05. Lord
              i. Ku,rioj. Some contemporaries of Jesus applied this name to
                 him as a form of polite address, just as the word "Sir."
                 However, especially after the resurrection it has another
                 meaning. It designates Jesus as the owner and ruler of the
                 Church. It can also stand for the name of God.


          06. Lamb of God
              i. This highlights Jesus' role as the sacrificial lamb of God
                 who takes away the sin of the world (John 1:29).


        j. How was Christ born?
          01. WSC 22: "Christ, the Son of God, became man, by taking to
              himself a true body, and a reasonable soul, being conceived
              by the power of the Holy Ghost, in the womb of the virgin
              Mary, and born of her yet without sin."


        k. Explain and defend (including Scripture proofs) the Virgin
           Birth.
          01. In Isaiah 7:14 says, "Therefore the Lord himself will give
              you a sign: The virgin will be with child and will give birth
              to a son, and will call him Immanuel."
          02. In Matthew 1:20-25 the angel tells Joseph that her pregnancy
              was from the Holy Spirit (1:20) and that this is in
              fulfillment of Isaiah 7;14 and the text says that Joseph had
              no union with her until she gave birth to Jesus.
          03. In Luke 1:26ff. Mary's response to the angel Gabriel who
              tells her that she will be with child and give birth to a son
              is to say, "'How will this be,'...'since I am a virgin?'"
              (Luke 1:34).
          04. Hebrews 4:15 says that Jesus was without sin. This could have
              only been the case if he had been born supernaturally, that
              is, without inheriting Adam's sin nature.


        l. Trace revelation of the person and work of Christ from the
           beginning of the Old Testament.
          01. Genesis 3:15 speaks of the seed of woman, which Paul later
              applies to Christ (Rom 16:20).
          02. Genesis 14:17-20. The appearance of Melchizedek to whom
              Abraham tithes and from whom Abraham receives a blessing
              foreshadows the priesthood of Christ (cf. Heb 7) and some
              have even seen this as a appearance of the pre-existent
              Christ.
          03. Genesis 49:10: "The scepter will not depart from Judah, nor
              the ruler's staff from between his feet, until he comes to
              whom it belongs and the obedience of the nations is his."
          04. Exodus 12. The Passover witnesses to Christ (cf. 1 Cor 5:7).
          05. Jesus work was prefigured in the sacrificial system (Lev 1-7)
              and his mediatorial role was prefigured in the priesthood
              (Lev 8-9).
          06. Deuteronomy 18:15 speaks of a prophet whom the Lord God would
              raise up and to whom the Israelites would have to listen (cf.
              Acts 7:37).
          07. 2 Samuel 7 speaks of an everlasting kingdom for David and a
              unique Father/Son relationship between God and one of David's
              descendants, which Hebrews 1:5 applies to Christ.
          08. Certain Psalms are Messianic, such as Psalm 2:7 (cf. Heb 1:5;
              5:5), and Psalm 110:1,4 (cf. Mat 22:41-46; Heb 5:6).
          09. Isaiah 53, the Suffering Servant.
          10. Jeremiah 31:31-34, the New Covenant.
          11. Ezekiel 34:20-24, God's Shepherd, his servant David, who will
              tend God's flock (cf. John 10).


        m. What is the humiliation of Christ?
          01. WSC 27: "Christ's humiliation consisted in his being born,
              and that in a low condition [a], made under the law [b],
              undergoing the miseries of this life [c], the wrath of God
              [d], and the cursed death of the cross [e]; in being buried,
              and continuing under the power of death for a time [f]."
                 [a]. Luke 2:7; II Cor. 8:9; Gal. 4:4
                 [b]. Gal. 4:4
                 [c]. Isa. 53:3; Luke 9:58; John 4:6; 11:35; Heb. 2:18
                 [d]. Ps. 22:1 (Matt. 27:46); Isa. 53:10; I John 2:2
                 [e]. Gal. 3:13; Phil. 2:8
                 [f]. Matt. 12:40; I Cor. 15:3-4


        n. Define and distinguish the active and passive obedience of
           Christ.
          01. Active obedience refers to the fact that "Christ had to live
              a life of perfect obedience to God in order to earn
              righteousness for us."[65]
              i. Romans 5:19: "For just as through the disobedience of the
                 one man the many were made sinners, so also through the
                 obedience of the one man the many will be made righteous."
          02. Passive obedience refers to Jesus' suffering and sacrificial
              death on our behalf which paid the penalty for sin (Mark
              10:45; Isaiah 53:6; Rom 3:25; 4:25).


        o. What happened in the resurrection of Christ?
              i. "The resurrection of Christ did not consist in the mere
                 fact that He came to life again, and that body and soul
                 were re-united. If this were all that it involved, He could
                 not be called 'the first-fruits of them that slept,' I Cor.
                 15:20, nor 'the firstborn of the dead,' Col. 1:18; Rev.
                 1:5, since others were restored to life before Him. It
                 consisted rather in this that in Him human nature, both
                 body and soul was restored to its pristine strength and
                 perfection and even raised to a higher level, while body
                 and soul were re-united in a living organism [cf. 1 Cor
                 15:42-44]...From the Gospel story we learn that the body of
                 Jesus had undergone a remarkable change, so that He was
                 easily recognized and could suddenly appear and disappear
                 in a surprising manner, Luke 24:31, 36; John 20:13, 19;
                 21:7; but that it was nevertheless a material and very real
                 body, Luke 24:39...Through the resurrection He became the
                 life-giving Spirit, I Cor. 15:45. The resurrection of
                 Christ had a threefold significance: (1) It constituted a
                 declaration of the Father that the last enemy had been
                 vanquished, the penalty paid, and the condition on which
                 life was promised, met. (2) It symbolized what was destined
                 to happen to the members of Christ's mystical body in their
                 justification, spiritual birth, and future blessed
                 resurrection, Rom. 6:4, 5, 9; 8:11; I Cor. 6:14; 15:20-22;
                 II Cor. 4:10, 11, 14; Col. 2:12; I Thess. 4:14. (3) It is
                 also connected instrumentally with their justification,
                 regeneration, and final resurrection; Rom. 4:25; 5:10; Eph.
                 1:20; Phil. 3:10; I Pet. 1:3."[66]


          01. Discuss "glorified body."
              i. The "glorified body" is the resurrected state whereby the
                 body is animated and guided wholly by the Spirit, is free
                 from sinful inclinations (1 Cor 6:13-14), and is
                 imperishable-free from sickness and decay-glorious, and
                 powerful (1 Cor 15:42-44).






        p. What are the offices of Christ?
              i. WSC 23: "Christ, as our Redeemer, executeth the offices of
                 a prophet, of a priest, and of a king, both in his estate
                 of humiliation and exaltation."


          01. How does Christ execute them?
              i. WSC 24: "Christ executeth the office of a prophet, in
                 revealing to us, by his Word and Spirit, the will of God
                 for our salvation."
             ii. WSC 25: "Christ executeth the office of a priest, in his
                 once offering up of himself a sacrifice to satisfy divine
                 justice, and reconcile us to God; and in making continual
                 intercession for us."
            iii. WSC 26: "Christ executeth the office of a king, in subduing
                 us to himself, in ruling and defending us, and in
                 restraining and conquering all his and our enemies."


        q. Who is the only redeemer of God's elect?
          01. WSC 21: "The only Redeemer of God's elect is the Lord Jesus
              Christ, who, being the eternal Son of God, became man and so
              was, and continueth to be, God and man in two distinct
              natures, and one person, forever."


        r. What is a redeemer?
          01. A redeemer is one who delivers someone else from captivity.


        s. What is the atonement?
          01. "The atonement is the work Christ did in his life and death
              to earn our salvation...Sometimes it is used to refer only to
              Jesus' dying and paying for our sins on the cross.
              But...since saying benefits also come to us from Christ's
              life, we have included that in our definition as well."[67]


        t. Was the atonement necessary? Explain and defend (include
           Scripture proofs).
